<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prompt tuning of vision-language models like CLIP is highly sensitive to mislabeled data; given CLIP's near-optimal initialization, adaptation should be conservative to avoid large gradient updates from noisy samples.

Method: Double-Softmax Prompt Tuning (DSPT) applies sequential probabilistic normalization (double softmax) to suppress gradients from high-error samples; no hyperparameter tuning required.

Result: Empirical results show state-of-the-art robustness across noisy benchmarks, outperforming methods with complex architectures and handcrafted hyperparameters; theoretical analysis and empirical evidence support adaptive gradient suppression.

Conclusion: DSPT turns gradient vanishing into a principled noise-filtering mechanism, providing a simple, drop-in, hyperparameter-free approach that enhances label-noise robustness in prompt tuning.

Abstract: Contrastive vision-language models like CLIP exhibit remarkable zero-shot generalization. However, prompt tuning remains highly sensitive to label noise, as mislabeled samples generate disproportionately large gradients that can overwhelm pre-trained priors. We argue that because CLIP already provides a near-optimal initialization, adaptation should be inherently conservative, particularly against the extreme gradient updates common in noisy settings. To this end, we propose Double-Softmax Prompt Tuning (DSPT), a hyperparameter-free method for intrinsic gradient suppression. By applying a sequential probabilistic normalization, DSPT induces a self-adaptive saturation zone that suppresses gradients from high-error noisy samples while maintaining informative updates. We also provide both theoretical analysis and empirical evidence about how this mechanism achieves adaptive suppression. This design transforms ``gradient vanishing'', traditionally a training bottleneck, into a principled noise-filtering shield for label-noise prompt tuning. Extensive experiments confirm that this simple, drop-in design achieves state-of-the-art robustness across various noisy benchmarks, outperforming methods with complex architectures and handcrafted hyperparameters.

*Hao Wei,Yanhui Zhou,Chenyang Ge,Saeed Anwar,Ajmal Mian*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: 1) Learnable reversible transformation enabling invertible downscaling and upscaling in the latent space (SVD-inspired). 2) Adaptive detail prior: a high-frequency dictionary capturing empirical averages of common structures to compensate quantization loss. 3) Lightweight pixel semantic embedder providing semantic conditioning for the pretrained diffusion model.

Result: Extensive experiments show FaithEIR consistently outperforms state-of-the-art methods in reconstruction fidelity and perceptual quality; code, model weights, and results are released.

Abstract: Most recent extreme rescaling methods struggle to preserve semantically consistent structures and produce realistic details, due to the severely ill-posed nature of low- to high-resolution mapping under scaling factors of $16\times$ or higher. To alleviate the above problems, we propose FaithEIR, a diffusion-based framework for extreme image rescaling. Inspired by singular value decomposition, we develop learnable reversible transformation that enables invertible downscaling and upscaling in the latent space. To compensate for information loss due to quantization, we propose an adaptive detail prior, a high-frequency dictionary that captures the empirical average of commonly occurring structures in the training data. Finally, we design a lightweight pixel semantic embedder to provide semantic conditioning for the pretrained diffusion model. We present extensive experimental results demonstrating that our FaithEIR consistently outperforms state-of-the-art methods, achieving superior reconstruction fidelity and perceptual quality. Our code, model weights, and detailed results are released at https://github.com/cshw2021/FaithEIR.

Unlike real videos that exhibit natural temporal fluctuations in cross-modal alignment due to semantic variations, AIGVs display unnaturally stable semantic trajectories governed by given input prompts. It is built on three key designs. Stochastic Condition Masking (SCM) randomly partitions modalities into clean conditions and noisy targets during training, enabling omni-directional conditional generation instead of fixed mappings. Decoupled Gated LoRA (DGL) introduces per-modality LoRAs that are activated when a modality serves as the generation target, preserving the strong priors of the VDM. Cross-Modal Self-Attention (CMSA) shares keys and values across modalities while keeping modality-specific queries, facilitating information exchange and inter-modal alignment. Method: 1) Build two complementary temporal graphs from static and dynamic visual features and apply Position-wise Nodes Alignment to fuse them. 2) Introduce Query-Clip Contrastive Learning to align clips with queries and Adaptive Graph Modeling for query-aware representations. 3) Incorporate multi-granularity temporal proposals with Progressive Easy-to-Hard Training to bridge coarse localization and fine boundary refinement.

Third, SDGAN incorporates multi-granularity temporal proposals within Progressive Easy-to-Hard Training Strategy, effectively bridging coarse-grained semantic localization and fine-grained temporal boundary refinement. ### [51] [PhysEdit: Physically-Consistent Region-Aware Image Editing via Adaptive Spatio-Temporal Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00707)
*Guandong Li,Mengxia Ye*

Adaptive computation across space and time is proposed as a key missing capability.

Method: Two inference-time modules compose without backbone retraining: (1) Complexity-Adaptive Reasoning Depth (CARD) predicts per-instruction edit complexity from the instruction and reference image and allocates reasoning steps N_r and reasoning-token length r per sample, turning inference into conditional computation; (2) Spatial Reasoning Mask (SRM) derives an instruction-conditioned spatial prior from cross-attention to confine reasoning to regions that semantically require it. Speedup is category-dependent, reaching 1.52x for appearance-level edits. A 30-sample pilot with full ablations isolates the contribution of each module.

Conclusion: Adaptive inference through CARD and SRM enables conditional computation without backbone retraining and yields meaningful efficiency gains with at least maintained or improved alignment to instructions and identity preservation. The approach validates adaptive spatial-temporal reasoning as a principal driver of efficiency and may generalize to other editing tasks.

Abstract: Image editing instructions are heterogeneous: a color swap, an object insertion, and a physical-action edit all demand different spatial coverage and different reasoning depth, yet existing reasoning-based editors apply a single fixed inference recipe to every instruction. PhysEdit introduces two inference-time modules that compose without retraining the backbone. At its core, (1) Complexity-Adaptive Reasoning Depth (CARD) predicts edit complexity directly from the instruction and reference image and allocates the reasoning step count N_r and reasoning-token length r per sample -- turning a previously fixed inference schedule into a conditional-computation problem. CARD is supported by (2) a Spatial Reasoning Mask (SRM) that extracts an instruction-conditioned spatial prior from cross-attention to confine reasoning to regions that semantically require it. On the full 737-case ImgEdit Basic-Edit Suite, PhysEdit delivers a 1.18x wall-clock speedup (64.3s vs. 76.1s per sample) over a strong reasoning baseline while slightly improving instruction adherence (CLIP-T 0.2283 vs. 0.2266, +0.7%) and matching identity preservation within noise (CLIP-I 0.8246 vs. 0.8280). The speedup is category-dependent and reaches 1.52x on appearance-level edits, validating CARD's adaptive allocation as the principal source of efficiency gain. A 30-sample pilot with full ablations isolates the contribution of each module.

In latter stage, we incorporate these rewards into the model's optimization process, constraining the optimization space and improving alignment between derained outputs and clean images. By leveraging IQA-based self-reinforced loss and dynamically updated rewards, we enhance the quality of synthesized pseudo-paired data and stabilize the optimization. Extensive experiments demonstrate that our method achieves SOTA performance across multiple datasets, including paired synthetic, paired real, and unpaired real images, outperforming existing unsupervised deraining approaches in both subjective and objective IQA metrics. Abstract: Single-point supervised infrared small target detection (IRSTD) drastically reduces dense annotation costs. In this paper, we study a minimalist alternative: generating point-to-mask supervision online through in-batch, point-anchored feature-affinity propagation. We instantiate this paradigm as GSACP, an end-to-end testbed that directly supervises the detector using hard-margin feature affinity gated by local image priors, entirely eliminating external label-evolution loops.
  This compact design, however, exposes an optimization bottleneck. Because the affinity target is generated from the same feature representation being optimized, training forms a self-referential loop. We theoretically formalize this as \emph{Self-Referential Propagation Drift}, a representation-supervision entanglement that can sharpen true boundaries or distort the feature space to satisfy its own targets. To systematically isolate these failure modes, we apply a protocolized single-variable ablation procedure spanning local EMA teacher decoupling, hard-background contrastive separation, and adaptive support geometry.
  On the SIRST3 dataset, GSACP-Final establishes a new ultra-low false-alarm operating regime, achieving a highly competitive $0.6674$ mIoU while demonstrating a $38\% relative reduction in false-positive artifacts ($\mathrm{Fa}$) compared with PAL. By systematically deconstructing the end-to-end paradigm, we map its performance boundaries and show that in-batch feature propagation provides a compact alternative for deployment scenarios where false-alarm suppression is paramount.

In this study, we present a quantum implementation of Sobel-based edge detection and Harris-style corner detection. Two quantum image encoding methods - Flexible Representation of Quantum Images (FRQI) and Quantum Probability Image Encoding (QPIE) - are used to encode the input data and are comparatively analyzed. The proposed approach introduces a quantum gradient computation scheme based on lag-2 differences, enabling the evaluation of gradient-like features in superposition. To improve detection quality and reduce false positives, a classical post-processing step is applied to candidate corner points identified by the quantum circuit. Results show that the proposed quantum circuits produce outputs consistent with classical Sobel and Harris operators. Furthermore, the QPIE-based configuration yields more stable and coherent results than FRQI, especially under limited measurement shots. While gradient computation can be performed efficiently at the circuit level, the overall cost remains dominated by state preparation, measurement, and classical post-processing. All experiments are conducted under noiseless simulation, and performance on NISQ hardware may be affected by noise and measurement limitations. Therefore, this work demonstrates a functional and scalable quantum realization of classical edge and corner detection methods rather than an end-to-end speedup.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: A multi-scale transformer where semantic prototype conditioning modulates the CLIP global embedding via four learned Banks (illumination, background, head pose, appearance) to form two context-biased global tokens. These tokens, the CLIP patch tokens, and CNN tokens are fused at the first layer. Each token passes through sparse Mixture-of-Experts modules to enable conditional computation. For cross-domain adaptation, an adversarial domain adaptation framework with a feature-separation loss encourages decorrelation between the two global tokens.

Abstract: Gaze estimation methods commonly use facial appearances to predict the direction of a person gaze. To address these challenges, we propose Globally-conditioned Multi-scale Gaze estimation (GMGaze), which leverages a multi-scale transformer architecture. Specifically, the model first introduces semantic prototype conditioning, which modulates the CLIP global image embedding using four learned prototype banks (i.e., illumination, background, head pose and appearance) to generate two complementary context-biased global tokens. These tokens, along with the CLIP patch and CNN tokens, are fused at the first layer. This early unified fusion prevents information loss common in late-stage merging. Finally, each token passes through sparse Mixture-of-Experts modules, providing conditional computational capacity without uniformly increasing dense parameters. For cross-domain adaptation, we incorporate an adversarial domain adaptation technique with a feature separation loss that encourages the two global tokens to remain de-correlated. Experiments using four public benchmarks (MPIIFaceGaze, EYEDIAP, Gaze360, and ETH-XGaze) show that GMGaze achieves mean angular errors of 2.49$^\circ$, 3.22$^\circ$, 10.16$^\circ$, and 1.44$^\circ$, respectively, outperforming previous baselines in all within-domain settings. Motivation: To better align vision encoders with autoregressive LLMs while keeping the architecture simple and data-efficient by using a standard language modeling objective instead of contrastive losses or an auxiliary text decoder.

Method: Pretrain a Vision Transformer to predict language tokens from visual tokens using a standard LM objective; no contrastive batch construction or extra text decoder; a single transformer jointly models visual and textual tokens; pretraining on 8B samples from Recap-DataComp-1B; further pretraining on multi-resolution images at native aspect ratios.

Result: GenLIP matches or surpasses strong multimodal baselines across diverse benchmarks while using substantially less pretraining data; continued pretraining on higher-resolution images yields improvements on detail-sensitive tasks such as OCR and chart understanding.

Conclusion: GenLIP is a simple, scalable, and effective foundation for vision encoders in multimodal LLMs, combining simplicity with strong performance and data efficiency; additional pretraining on multi-resolution data enhances fine-grained understanding capabilities.

Abstract: In this paper, we present \textbf{Gen}erative \textbf{L}anguage-\textbf{I}mage \textbf{P}re-training (GenLIP), a minimalist generative pretraining framework for Vision Transformers (ViTs) designed for multimodal large language models (MLLMs). To better align vision encoders with the autoregressive nature of LLMs, GenLIP trains a ViT to predict language tokens directly from visual tokens using a standard language modeling objective, without contrastive batch construction or an additional text decoder. This design offers three key advantages: (1) \textbf{Simplicity}: a single transformer jointly models visual and textual tokens; (2) \textbf{Scalability}: it scales effectively with both data and model size; and (3) \textbf{Performance}: it achieves competitive or superior results across diverse multimodal benchmarks. Trained on 8B samples from Recap-DataComp-1B, GenLIP matches or surpasses strong baselines despite using substantially less pretraining data. After continued pretraining on multi-resolution images at native aspect ratios, GenLIP further improves on detail-sensitive tasks such as OCR and chart understanding, making it a strong foundation for vision encoders in MLLMs.

</details>


### [62] [Posterior Augmented Flow Matching](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00825)
*George Stoica,Sayak Paul,Matthew Wallingford,Vivek Ramanujan,Abhay Nori,Winson Han,Ali Farhadi,Ranjay Krishna,Judy Hoffman*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: PAFM generalizes Flow Matching by augmenting supervision with a posterior over valid target completions. It factorizes the intractable posterior into a likelihood and a prior, using importance sampling to create a mixture of plausible targets. This yields an unbiased FM objective with substantially reduced gradient variance, mitigating flow collapse. Empirically, it improves FID50K up to 3.4 points across multiple model scales and architectures on ImageNet and CC12M, with negligible compute overhead. Code available at the provided repo.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Flow Matching (FM) uses a single-target supervision per training sample, which is highly sparse and leads to high gradient variance and potential flow collapse for high-dimensional data. A richer supervisory signal over multiple plausible continuations could stabilize training and improve generalization.

Method: Introduce Posterior-Augmented Flow Matching (PAFM) by replacing single-target supervision with an expectation over an approximate posterior of target completions given an intermediate state and condition. Factorize this posterior into (i) the likelihood of the intermediate under a hypothesized endpoint and (ii) the prior probability of that endpoint under the condition. Use importance sampling to construct a mixture over multiple candidate targets, yielding an unbiased estimator of the FM objective while reducing gradient variance by aggregating information from many plausible continuations.

Result: PAFM achieves up to 3.4 FID50K improvements over FM across varying model scales (SiT-B/2, SiT-XL/2) and architectures (SiT, MMDiT), in both class- and text-conditioned benchmarks (ImageNet and CC12M), with negligible compute overhead.

Conclusion: PAFM provides a theoretically grounded generalization of Flow Matching that mitigates flow collapse by aggregating multiple plausible target continuations through importance sampling. It remains unbiased with respect to the original FM objective and offers tangible empirical gains with minimal extra compute; code is released.

Abstract: Flow matching (FM) trains a time-dependent vector field that transports samples from a simple prior to a complex data distribution. However, for high-dimensional images, each training sample supervises only a single trajectory and intermediate point, yielding an extremely sparse and high-variance training signal. This under-constrained supervision can cause flow collapse, where the learned dynamics memorize specific source-target pairings, mapping diverse inputs to overly similar outputs, failing to generalize. We introduce Posterior-Augmented Flow Matching (PAFM), a theoretically grounded generalization of FM that replaces single-target supervision with an expectation over an approximate posterior of valid target completions for a given intermediate state and condition. PAFM factorizes this intractable posterior into (i) the likelihood of the intermediate under a hypothesized endpoint and (ii) the prior probability of that endpoint under the condition, and uses an importance sampling scheme to construct a mixture over multiple candidate targets. We prove that PAFM yields an unbiased estimator of the original FM objective while substantially reducing gradient variance during training by aggregating information from many plausible continuation trajectories per intermediate. Finally, we show that PAFM improves over FM by up to 3.4 FID50K across different model scales (SiT-B/2 and SiT-XL/2), different architectures (SiT and MMDiT), and in both class and text conditioned benchmarks (ImageNet and CC12M), with a negligible increase in the compute overhead. Code: https://github.com/gstoica27/PAFM.git.

</details>

### [63] [AgentReputation: A Decentralized Agentic AI Reputation Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00073)
*Mohd Sameen Chishti,Damilare Peter Oyinloye,Jingyue Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes AgentReputation, a three-layer, decentralized reputation framework for agentic AI systems to address strategic evaluation, cross-domain transferability, and verification rigor variability in decentralized AI marketplaces; introduces context-conditioned reputation cards, verification regimes, and a policy engine, with future research directions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current reputation mechanisms fail in decentralized, agentic AI marketplaces due to (1) strategic manipulation of evaluations, (2) poor cross-context transfer of competence, and (3) inconsistent verification rigor. A robust, decoupled architecture is needed to enable reliable reputation signals across heterogeneous tasks.

Method: Introduce a three-layer architecture: (1) task execution, (2) reputation services, (3) tamper-proof persistence. Implement explicit verification regimes linked to reputation metadata, context-conditioned reputation cards to prevent cross-domain conflation, and a decision-facing policy engine for resource allocation, access control, and adaptive verification escalation. Outline future research directions (verification ontologies, strength quantification, privacy-preserving evidence, cold-start bootstrapping, adversarial defenses).

Result: A novel, decentralized reputation framework (AgentReputation) with modular separation of concerns to improve reliability of reputation signals in agentic AI systems, plus a roadmap of verification and governance features and future research.

Conclusion: AgentReputation enables independent evolution of execution, reputation, and persistence layers, with explicit verification tied to metadata and context-specific reputation cards; sets a foundation for robust, adaptable reputation in decentralized AI markets and identifies avenues for verification-focused research and defense against manipulation.

Abstract: Decentralized, agentic AI marketplaces are rapidly emerging to support software engineering tasks such as debugging, patch generation, and security auditing, often operating without centralized oversight. However, existing reputation mechanisms fail in this setting for three fundamental reasons: agents can strategically optimize against evaluation procedures; demonstrated competence does not reliably transfer across heterogeneous task contexts; and verification rigor varies widely, from lightweight automated checks to costly expert review. Current approaches to reputation drawing on federated learning, blockchain-based AI platforms, and large language model safety research are unable to address these challenges in combination. We therefore propose \textbf{AgentReputation}, a decentralized, three-layer reputation framework for agentic AI systems. The framework separates task execution, reputation services, and tamper-proof persistence to both leverage their respective strengths and enable independent evolution. The framework introduces explicit verification regimes linked to agent reputation metadata, as well as context-conditioned reputation cards that prevent reputation conflation across domains and task types. In addition, AgentReputation provides a decision-facing policy engine that supports resource allocation, access control, and adaptive verification escalation based on risk and uncertainty. Building on this framework, we outline several future research directions, including the development of verification ontologies, methods for quantifying verification strength, privacy-preserving evidence mechanisms, cold-start reputation bootstrapping, and defenses against adversarial manipulation.

### [64] [Minimal, Local, Causal Explanations for Jailbreak Success in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00123)
*Shubham Kumar,Narendra Ahuja*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LOCA provides local, causal explanations for why a jailbreak succeeds in LLMs by identifying a minimal set of interpretable intermediate representations whose manipulation induces model refusal; it outperforms global explanation baselines, typically needing around six changes (vs over 20 for prior methods) to trigger refusal; evaluated on Gemma and Llama chat models; code will be released.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To move beyond global explanations of jailbreaks and offer local, causal explanations for each successful jailbreak, acknowledging that different jailbreaks and harmful categories may rely on different intermediate concepts.

Method: LOCA identifies a minimal, interpretable set of intermediate representation changes that causally induce the model to refuse on a given jailbroken prompt, providing a local, causal explanation for why that particular jailbreak succeeded; evaluated against a large jailbreak benchmark across Gemma and Llama chat models, compared to prior methods.

Result: LOCA typically induces refusal with about six targeted changes; prior methods often fail to achieve refusal even after 20 changes; demonstrates effective local causal explanations for jailbreak success across multiple models.

Conclusion: LOCA advances mechanistic, local explanations of jailbreak success in LLMs; supports targeted defense implications and interpretability research; code is slated for release.

Abstract: Safety trained large language models (LLMs) can often be induced to answer harmful requests through jailbreak prompts. Because we lack a robust understanding of why LLMs are susceptible to jailbreaks, future frontier models operating more autonomously in higher-stakes settings may similarly be vulnerable to such attacks. Prior work has studied jailbreak success by examining the model's intermediate representations, identifying directions in this space that causally encode concepts like harmfulness and refusal. Then, they globally explain all jailbreak attacks as attempting to reduce or strengthen these concepts (e.g., reduce harmfulness). However, different jailbreak strategies may succeed by strengthening or suppressing different intermediate concepts, and the same jailbreak strategy may not work for different harmful request categories (e.g., violence vs. cyberattack); thus, we seek to give a local explanation -- i.e., why did this specific jailbreak succeed? To address this gap, we introduce LOCA, a method that gives Local, CAusal explanations of jailbreak success by identifying a minimal set of interpretable, intermediate representation changes that causally induce model refusal on an otherwise successful jailbreak request. We evaluate LOCA on harmful original-jailbreak pairs from a large jailbreak benchmark across Gemma and Llama chat models, comparing against prior methods adapted to this setting. LOCA can successfully induce refusal by making, on average, six interpretable changes; prior work routinely fails to achieve refusal even after 20 changes. LOCA is a step toward mechanistic, local explanations of jailbreak success in LLMs. Code to be released.

### [65] [Are Tools All We Need? Unveiling the Tool-Use Tax in LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00136)
*Kaituo Zhang,Zhen Xiong,Mingyu Zhong,Zhimeng Jiang,Zhouyuan Yuan,Zhecheng Li,Ying Lin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Tool-augmented reasoning is not universally superior; semantic distractors reveal a 'tool-use tax' that can negate gains; a Factorized Intervention Framework uncovers cost components and a gating approach (G-STEP) helps reduce protocol-induced errors, but true gains require stronger intrinsic reasoning and tool interaction.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Questioning the widely held assumption that external tools reliably improve reasoning and reliability; quantify the cost-benefit of tool use and understand the impact of semantic noise.

Method: Proposes a Factorized Intervention Framework that separates costs from prompt formatting, tool-calling protocol, and actual tool execution gain; conducts analysis under semantic noise; introduces G-STEP as an inference-time gate to mitigate protocol-induced errors.

Result: Under semantic noise, benefits from tool use often fail to offset the overhead—the 'tool-use tax'; G-STEP yields partial recovery; substantial improvements require strengthening intrinsic reasoning and tool-interaction capabilities.

Conclusion: Tool use alone is not a universal fix; to improve robustness, enhance the model's internal reasoning and interaction with tools; gating helps but is insufficient for large gains.

Abstract: Tool-augmented reasoning has become a popular direction for LLM-based agents, and it is widely assumed to improve reasoning and reliability. However, we demonstrate that this consensus does not always hold: in the presence of semantic distractors, tool-augmented reasoning does not necessarily outperform native CoT. To explain this performance gap, we propose a Factorized Intervention Framework that isolates the cost of prompt formatting, the overhead of the tool-calling protocol, and the actual gain from executing tools. Our analysis reveals a critical tradeoff: under semantic noise, the gains from tools often fail to offset the "tool-use tax", which is the performance degradation introduced by the tool-calling protocol itself. To address this, we introduce G-STEP, a lightweight inference-time gate to mitigate protocol-induced errors. While this yields partial recovery, our findings suggest that more substantial improvements still require strengthening the model's intrinsic reasoning and tool-interaction capabilities.

### [66] [TUR-DPO: Topology- and Uncertainty-Aware Direct Preference Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00224)
*Abdulhady Abas Abdullah,Fatemeh Daneshfar,Seyedali Mirjalili,Mourad Oussalah*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A topology- and uncertainty-aware extension of Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) for RLHF, TUR-DPO, improves alignment by evaluating reasoning topologies and calibrated uncertainty, yielding better faithfulness, calibration, and judge win-rates without RL-based rollout.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: DPO treats preferences as flat winner/loser signals and is sensitive to noisy, brittle reasoning, limiting alignment quality; RLHF with PPO adds complexity and requires online rollouts. There is a need for a stable, RL-free method that accounts for the structure of reasoning.

Method: Introduce lightweight reasoning topologies and combine semantic faithfulness, utility, and topology quality into a calibrated uncertainty signal. Factorize a small learnable reward over these signals and incorporate it into an uncertainty-weighted DPO objective that remains RL-free and uses a fixed or moving reference policy.

Result: Empirically, across 7–8B models and diverse benchmarks (math reasoning, factual QA, summarization, helpful/harmless dialogue), TUR-DPO improves judge win-rates, faithfulness, and calibration relative to DPO, while preserving training simplicity and avoiding online rollouts. Gains in multimodal and long-context settings; matches or exceeds PPO on reasoning-centric tasks.

Conclusion: TUR-DPO offers a practical, stable, RL-free alignment method that leverages topology and uncertainty to outperform plain DPO and rival PPO on reasoning tasks, with broad applicability and preserved simplicity.

Abstract: Aligning large language models (LLMs) with human preferences is commonly done via reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) with Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) or, more simply, via Direct Preference Optimization (DPO). While DPO is stable and RL-free, it treats preferences as flat winner vs. loser signals and is sensitive to noisy or brittle preferences arising from fragile chains of thought. We propose TUR-DPO, a topology- and uncertainty-aware variant of DPO that rewards how answers are derived, not only what they say, by eliciting lightweight reasoning topologies and combining semantic faithfulness, utility, and topology quality into a calibrated uncertainty signal. A small learnable reward is factorized over these signals and incorporated into an uncertainty-weighted DPO objective that remains RL-free and relies only on a fixed or moving reference policy. Empirically, across open 7-8B models and benchmarks spanning mathematical reasoning, factual question answering, summarization, and helpful/harmless dialogue, TUR-DPO improves judge win-rates, faithfulness, and calibration relative to DPO while preserving training simplicity and avoiding online rollouts. We further observe consistent gains in multimodal and long-context settings, and show that TUR-DPO matches or exceeds PPO on reasoning-centric tasks while maintaining operational simplicity.

### [68] [Causal Foundations of Collective Agency](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00248)
*Frederik Hytting Jørgensen,Sebastian Weichwald,Lewis Hammond*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A behavioral definition of collective agency using causal games and causal abstraction, enabling analysis of when a group acts as a unified agent; applied to actor-critic incentives and voting mechanisms; aims to guide understanding and control of emergent collectives in multi-agent AI.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safety and foundational understanding: whether groups of agents form a unified agent; necessary for predicting and controlling emergent collective behavior in AI systems.

Method: Formalize collective agency via causal games (strategic multi-agent causal models) and causal abstraction (when a high-level model faithfully captures a low-level one); apply to actor-critic incentives puzzle and quantify collective agency in voting mechanisms.

Result: The framework solves a puzzle about multi-agent incentives in actor-critic models and yields quantitative assessments of collective agency across different voting mechanisms.

Conclusion: Provides a theoretical/empirical foundation to study, predict, and control emergent collective agents in multi-agent AI systems.

Abstract: A key challenge for the safety of advanced AI systems is the possibility that multiple simpler agents might inadvertently form a collective agent with capabilities and goals distinct from those of any individual. More generally, determining when a group of agents can be viewed as a unified collective agent is a foundational question in the study of interactions and incentives in both biological and artificial systems. We adopt a behavioral perspective in answering this question, ascribing collective agency to a group when viewing the group's joint actions as rational and goal-directed successfully predicts its behavior. We formalize this perspective on collective agency using causal games -- which are causal models of strategic, multi-agent interactions -- and causal abstraction -- which formalizes when a simple, high-level model faithfully captures a more complex, low-level model. We use this framework to solve a puzzle regarding multi-agent incentives in actor-critic models and to make quantitative assessments of the degree of collective agency exhibited by different voting mechanisms. Our framework aims to provide a foundation for theoretical and empirical work to understand, predict, and control emergent collective agents in multi-agent AI systems.

*Yuxuan Gao,Megan Wang,Yi Ling Yu*

TL;DR: TokenArena is a continuous, endpoint-level benchmark that measures AI inference across provider/model/SKU endpoints on five axes (speed, TTF, price, effective context, and quality), combining them into joules per correct answer, dollars per correct answer, and endpoint fidelity; it emphasizes empirical methodology over a single ranking and provides provenance and CC BY 4.0 release.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Public benchmarks often compare AI at the model or provider level, but real deployment decisions are made at the endpoint. TokenArena targets endpoint granularity to reveal performance and cost variations across deployments and presets.

Method: Evaluate 78 endpoints across 12 model families on five axes (output speed, time to first token, workload-blended price, effective context, quality on live endpoint); synthesize into three composites (energy per correct answer, money per correct answer, endpoint fidelity); include modeled energy estimates; analyze how workload presets reorder rankings; release framework, probe, eval harness, and v1.0 leaderboard under CC BY 4.0.

Result: Demonstrates substantial endpoint-to-endpoint variability: the same model shows up to 12.5-point accuracy differences on math/code, up to 12-point changes in fingerprint similarity to first-party references, tail latency differing by orders of magnitude, and energy-per-answer differing by a factor of 6.2; workload-aware pricing changes leaderboard topology (7/10 top under chat drop under retrieval-augmented; reasoning presets elevate frontier closed models); establishes TokenArena as a reproducible, methodology-driven benchmark with full provenance and replication invitation.

Conclusion: TokenArena provides a practical, end-to-end endpoint benchmarking framework that complements model/provider benchmarks by exposing deployment-level tradeoffs and enabling cross-endpoint comparisons; its methodological novelty, openness, and energy/cost metrics offer a richer lens for deployment decisions, though results depend on presets and modeled energy assumptions and require ongoing replication and evolution.

Abstract: Public inference benchmarks compare AI systems at the model and provider level, but the unit at which deployment decisions are actually made is the endpoint: the (provider, model, stock-keeping-unit) tuple at which a specific quantization, decoding strategy, region, and serving stack is exposed. We introduce TokenArena, a continuous benchmark that measures inference at endpoint granularity along five core axes (output speed, time to first token, workload-blended price, effective context, and quality on the live endpoint) and synthesizes them, together with a modeled energy estimate, into three headline composites: joules per correct answer, dollars per correct answer, and endpoint fidelity (output-distribution similarity to a first-party reference). The framework's novelty is empirical and methodological. Across 78 endpoints serving 12 model families, the same model on different endpoints differs in mean accuracy by up to 12.5 points on math and code, in fingerprint similarity to first party by up to 12 points, in tail latency by an order of magnitude, and in modeled joules per correct answer by a factor of 6.2. We further show that workload-aware blended pricing reorders the leaderboard substantially: 7 of 10 top-ranked endpoints under the chat preset (3:1 input:output) fall out of the top 10 under the retrieval-augmented preset (20:1), and the reasoning preset (1:5) elevates frontier closed models that the chat preset penalizes on price. We release the framework, schema, probe and eval harness, and a v1.0 leaderboard snapshot under CC BY 4.0. TokenArena is a methodology, not a single ranking; we publish full provenance and limitations and welcome external replication.

### [71] [AgentFloor: How Far Up the tool use Ladder Can Small Open-Weight Models Go?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00334)
*Ranit Karmakar,Jayita Chatterjee*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgentFloor introduces a deterministic 30-task benchmark with a six-tier capability ladder to map which agentic tasks truly require large frontier models versus smaller open-weight models. Short-horizon, structured tool-use tasks are largely solvable by small/mid open-weight models; the strongest open-weight model can match GPT-5 on the benchmark, while long-horizon planning still benefits from frontier models. The boundary is not solely scale-dependent and some failures are model-specific. Practically, use small models for routine actions and reserve frontier models for tasks demanding deep planning. Benchmark and resources released.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address a practical, production-relevant routing question: which parts of an agent workflow need deep frontier intelligence and which can be handled by smaller models? Existing evaluations do not directly quantify this boundary, especially under persistent constraints and long-horizon planning.

Method: A deterministic 30-task benchmark arranged as a six-tier capability ladder spanning instruction following, tool use, multi-step coordination, and long-horizon planning under persistent constraints. Evaluates 16 open-weight models (0.27B–32B) and GPT-5 across 16,542 scored runs. Analyzes performance across task classes and failure modes to delineate the boundary between model classes and interprets scale-related effects as non-universal. Includes release of benchmark, harness, sweep configurations, and run corpus.

Result: Small and mid-sized open-weight models excel at short-horizon, structured tool use that dominates real agent pipelines. The strongest open-weight model matches GPT-5 on the benchmark and is cheaper/faster. Long-horizon planning under persistent constraints remains a frontier area where frontier models have a clear advantage, with neither side achieving strong reliability. Boundary effects are not explained by scale alone; some improvements arise from targeted interventions but effects are model-specific.

Conclusion: Adopt a mixed-model strategy: deploy smaller open-weight models for routine actions and use large frontier models selectively for tasks demanding deeper planning and control. The work provides a practical design principle and release resources to facilitate benchmarking across agentic systems.

Abstract: Production agentic systems make many model calls per user request, and most of those calls are short, structured, and routine. This raises a practical routing question that existing evaluations do not directly answer: which parts of an agent workflow truly require large frontier intelligence, and which can be handled by smaller models? We introduce AgentFloor, a deterministic 30-task benchmark organized as a six-tier capability ladder, spanning instruction following, tool use, multi-step coordination, and long-horizon planning under persistent constraints. We evaluate 16 open-weight models, from 0.27B to 32B parameters, alongside GPT-5 across 16,542 scored runs. Our results reveal a clear boundary of model necessity. Small and mid-sized open-weight models are already sufficient for much of the short-horizon, structured tool use work that dominates real agent pipelines, and in aggregate, the strongest open-weight model matches GPT-5 on our benchmark while being substantially cheaper and faster to run. The gap appears most clearly on long-horizon planning tasks that require sustained coordination and reliable constraint tracking over many steps, where frontier models still hold an advantage, though neither side reaches strong reliability. We also find that this boundary is not explained by scale alone: some failures respond to targeted interventions, but the effects are model-specific rather than universal. These findings suggest a practical design principle for agentic systems: use smaller open-weight models for the broad base of routine actions, and reserve large frontier models for the narrower class of tasks that truly demand deeper planning and control. We release the benchmark, harness, sweep configurations, and full run corpus.

### [72] [Physically Native World Models: A Hamiltonian Perspective on Generative World Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00412)
*Sen Cui,Jingheng Ma*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Propose Hamiltonian World Models: encode observations into a structured latent phase space, evolve with Hamiltonian-inspired dynamics (with control, dissipation, and residual terms), decode predictions into future observations, and use rollouts for planning to achieve physically meaningful, actionable, long-horizon predictions; discuss benefits and practical challenges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current world-model paradigms (2D video generation, 3D scene-centric models, and JEPA-like latent models) often fail to provide physically reliable, controllable, long-horizon predictions for embodied decision making; a physically grounded approach is needed.

Method: Encode observations into a structured latent phase space; evolve the latent state via Hamiltonian-inspired dynamics with control, dissipation, and residual terms; decode the predicted trajectory into future observations; utilize the rollouts for planning; discuss how Hamiltonian structure could improve interpretability, data efficiency, and long-horizon stability, and address practical challenges such as friction, contact, non-conservative forces, and deformable objects.

Result: The framework is proposed to yield improved interpretability, data efficiency, and long-horizon stability; the abstract outlines benefits and practical challenges but does not report empirical results.

Conclusion: Hamiltonian World Models provide a physically grounded perspective intended to address bottlenecks in world-models, while acknowledging real-world robotic complexities that must be tackled in future work.

Abstract: World models have recently re-emerged as a central paradigm for embodied intelligence, robotics, autonomous driving, and model-based reinforcement learning. However, current world model research is often dominated by three partially separated routes: 2D video-generative models that emphasize visual future synthesis, 3D scene-centric models that emphasize spatial reconstruction, and JEPA-like latent models that emphasize abstract predictive representations. While each route has made important progress, they still struggle to provide physically reliable, action-controllable, and long-horizon stable predictions for embodied decision making. In this paper, we argue that the bottleneck of world models is no longer only whether they can generate realistic futures, but whether those futures are physically meaningful and useful for action. We propose \emph{Hamiltonian World Models} as a physically grounded perspective on world modeling. The key idea is to encode observations into a structured latent phase space, evolve the latent state through Hamiltonian-inspired dynamics with control, dissipation, and residual terms, decode the predicted trajectory into future observations, and use the resulting rollouts for planning. We discuss how Hamiltonian structure may improve interpretability, data efficiency, and long-horizon stability, while also noting practical challenges in real-world robotic scenes involving friction, contact, non-conservative forces, and deformable objects.

</details>


### [73] [AEM: Adaptive Entropy Modulation for Multi-Turn Agentic Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00425)

Abstract: Reinforcement learning (RL) has significantly advanced the ability of large language model (LLM) agents to interact with environments and solve multi-turn tasks. This paper presents AEM, a supervision-free credit assignment method that adaptively modulates entropy dynamics during RL training to achieve a more effective exploration-exploitation trade-off. Theoretically, we elevate entropy analysis from the token level to the response level to reduce token sampling variance and show that entropy drift under natural gradients is intrinsically governed by the product of the advantage and the relative response surprisal. Specifically, we derive a practical proxy to reshape training dynamics, enabling a natural transition from exploration to exploitation.   <summary>Details</summary>

Conclusion: An explicit interleaved textual-visual trace is crucial for achieving strong long-horizon robotic manipulation performance. The trace provides a structured, global plan that combines semantic goals with geometric cues, enabling a single multimodal model to generate, store, and reuse planning information for closed-loop control. Abstract: Long-horizon robotic manipulation requires plans that are both logically coherent and geometrically grounded. Existing Vision-Language-Action policies usually hide planning in latent states or expose only one modality: text-only chain-of-thought encodes causal order but misses spatial constraints, while visual prediction provides geometric cues but often remains local and semantically underconstrained. We introduce Interleaved Vision--Language Reasoning (IVLR), a policy framework built around \trace{}, an explicit intermediate representation that alternates textual subgoals with visual keyframes over the full task horizon. At test time, a single native multimodal transformer self-generates this global semantic-geometric trace from the initial observation and instruction, caches it, and conditions a closed-loop action decoder on the trace, original instruction, and current observation. Because standard robot datasets lack such traces, we construct pseudo-supervision by temporally segmenting demonstrations and captioning each stage with a vision-language model. Across simulated benchmarks for long-horizon manipulation and visual distribution shift, \method{} reaches 95.5\% average success on LIBERO, including 92.4\% on LIBERO-Long, and 59.4\% overall success on SimplerEnv-WidowX. Ablations show that both modalities are necessary: without traces, LIBERO-Long success drops to 37.7\%; text-only and vision-only traces reach 62.0\% and 68.4\%, while the full interleaved trace reaches 92.4\%. First, it constructs a visually enriched privileged context for the teacher using a target bounding box and a Gaussian soft mask, providing informative guidance without leaking exact coordinates. Second, it employs entropy-guided distillation, which adaptively weights tokens based on digit significance and teacher confidence, concentrating optimization on the most impactful and reliable positions. Our analysis combines two complementary lenses: a normative perspective that infers true need and utility from an optimal allocation of tool calls, and a descriptive perspective that infers the model's self-perceived need and utility from their observed behaviors. Making LLMs themselves explicitly Bayesian belief-updating engines remains computationally intensive and conceptually nontrivial as a general modeling target. In contrast, this paper argues that coherent decision-making requires Bayesian principles at the orchestration level of the agentic system, not necessarily the LLM agent parameters. ### [80] [Upward-Planar Drawings with Bounded Span](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00603)
*Patrizio Angelini,Sabine Cornelsen,Giordano Da Lozzo,Fabrizio Frati,Philipp Kindermann,Ignaz Rutter,Johannes Zink*

Main category: cs.CG

TL;DR: The work studies minimizing the vertical span in upward-planar layered drawings, giving combinatorial bounds for directed trees and a range of algorithmic results: NP-completeness for directed trees and biconnected single-source graphs; tractable cases for restricted families (few sources, prescribed embeddings); and fixed-parameter tractability with respect to vertex cover and treedepth plus span.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Understanding the trade-off between drawing aesthetics (small span) and computational feasibility in upward, layered graph drawings; relevant to graph drawing and visualization where vertical distance corresponds to "importance" or layering.

Method: Combinatorial analysis to derive span bounds for directed trees; complexity reductions to prove NP-completeness for directed trees and biconnected single-source graphs; design of efficient algorithms for families with a bounded number of sources (e.g., st-planar, prescribed embedding); parameterized complexity analysis showing FPT with respect to vertex cover number and with treedepth together with the span.

Result: Upper and lower bounds on the span for directed trees; NP-completeness of the span problem for directed trees and for biconnected single-source graphs; efficient algorithms for restricted families with few sources; the problem is fixed-parameter tractable when parameterized by vertex cover and by treedepth together with the span.

Conclusion: Span minimization in upward-planar graphs is hard in general but tractable for several natural restricted classes, and it admits FPT algorithms for certain parameters, inviting further study of additional graph families and tighter bounds.

Abstract: We consider upward-planar layered drawings of directed graphs, i.e., crossing-free drawings in which each edge is drawn as a y-monotone curve going upward from its tail to its head, and the y-coordinates of the vertices are integers. The span of an edge in such a drawing is the absolute difference between the y-coordinates of its endpoints, and the span of the drawing is the maximum span of any edge. The span of an upward-planar graph is the minimum span over all its upward-planar drawings.
  We study the problem of determining the span of upward-planar graphs and provide both combinatorial and algorithmic results. On the combinatorial side, we present upper and lower bounds for the span of directed trees. On the algorithmic side, we show that the problem of determining the span of an upward-planar graph is NP-complete already for directed trees and for biconnected single-source graphs. Moreover, we give efficient algorithms for several graph families with a bounded number of sources, including st-planar graphs and graphs where the planar or upward-planar embedding is prescribed. Furthermore, we show that the problem is fixed-parameter tractable with respect to the vertex cover number and the treedepth plus the span.

*Kevin Buchin,Mark Joachim Krallmann,Frank Staals*

Main category: cs.CG

TL;DR: A simpler 2D-geometry approach for MEC under axis-aligned queries using farthest-point Voronoi diagrams; achieves O(log^4 n) deterministic and O(log^{5/2} n log log n) randomized queries with O(n log^2 n) preprocessing.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Query processing leverages the combinatorial structure of the farthest-point Voronoi diagram to locate the constrained MEC efficiently.

Conclusion: Demonstrates that 2D farthest-point Voronoi diagrams suffice to achieve improved query performance for axis-aligned constrained MEC without 3D lifting or dualization, indicating potential applicability to other range-constrained geometric queries.

Abstract: Let $S$ be a set of $n$ points in $\mathbb{R}^2$. Our goal is to preprocess $S$ to efficiently compute the smallest enclosing disk of the points in $S$ that lie inside an axis-aligned query rectangle. Previous data structures for this problem achieve a query time of $O(\log^6 n)$ with $O(n \log^2 n)$ preprocessing time and space by lifting the points to 3D, dualizing them into polyhedra, and searching through their intersections. We present a significantly simpler approach, solely based on 2D geometric structures, specifically 2D farthest-point Voronoi diagrams. Our approach achieves a deterministic query time of $O(\log^4 n)$ and, via randomization, an expected query time of $O(\log^{5/2} n \log\log n)$ with the same preprocessing bounds.

TL;DR: Two physics-based interpretability metrics for MoCap-to-radar models; one aligns predictions with physics-derived Doppler frequency, the other tests velocity-frequency consistency under velocity perturbations. Conclusion: A physics-based interpretability framework reveals that accuracy alone is insufficient for physical fidelity in MoCap-to-radar mappings; temporal dynamics, especially temporal attention in transformer models, are important to capture the physics; suggests the need for physics-aware evaluation beyond traditional reconstruction metrics.

We introduce a physics-based interpretability framework to answer this question via two proposed complementary metrics: one measures alignment between model predictions and the physics-derived Doppler frequency, while the other tests whether predictions preserve the velocity-frequency relationship under velocity intervention. Both metrics require only MoCap input and model predictions, without access to measured radar data. Experiments across several model architectures reveal that low reconstruction error does not guarantee physical consistency: some, but not all, models achieve low error yet perform poorly on the two physics-based metrics. Further analysis shows that temporal attention is critical for transformer-based models to learn the underlying physics.

The Gaussian copula gave the most reliable results: stacked ensemble $R^2 = 0.96$ (RMSE $= 0.19$), with other learners maintaining high accuracy. Copula-based models improved residuals and produced spatially plausible maps. DBSCAN clustering revealed Fe and Mn as primary HPI contributors, consistent with regional hydrogeochemistry. Limitations include reliance on random (not spatial) cross-validation and basin-specific scope. Future work should explore spatial validation and other geological settings. ### [88] [Information-Theoretic Generalization Bounds for Stochastic Gradient Descent with Predictable Virtual Noise](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00064)
*Mohammad Partohaghighi*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Introduces history-adaptive virtual perturbations for SGD to obtain information-theoretic generalization bounds that account for adaptive, path-dependent geometry of gradient statistics, while preserving the SGD trajectory.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing virtual-perturbation bounds use fixed, history-independent perturbations, which fail to capture geometry induced by moving gradients, preconditioners, and curvature proxies. This limits the tightness and applicability of the bounds to realistic, adaptive optimization practices.

Method: Define perturbation covariance at each iteration as a function of past SGD history (predictable) but independent of current/future randomness. Employ a conditional Gaussian relative-entropy argument to derive bounds with adaptive geometry. Replace fixed sensitivity and gradient-deviation terms with conditional adaptive terms, add an output-sensitivity penalty from accumulated perturbations, and, under conditional unbiasedness, reduce deviation to a conditional variance. Separate local Gaussian smoothing from a global reference kernel to handle data-dependent adaptivity, introducing a covariance-comparison cost (KL price) for using a different admissible reference geometry. Recover fixed-noise bounds under synchronization (deterministic/public/prefix-observable covariances). The framework thus generalizes virtual perturbation analysis to history-dependent SGD without algorithm modification and subsumes fixed isotropic and geometry-aware bounds as special cases.

Result: Derives generalization bounds for SGD with history-adaptive perturbations, including an output-sensitivity penalty and a covariance-comparison cost. The bounds interpolate between fixed-noise and geometry-aware regimes and reduce to conventional results under synchronization assumptions, thereby extending the applicability of information-theoretic generalization bounds to adaptive, path-dependent SGD.

Conclusion: The proposed framework enables history-dependent, adaptive perturbations within information-theoretic generalization analysis, bridging the gap between fixed-noise bounds and geometry-aware analyses. It preserves the SGD trajectory, accommodates data-dependent adaptivity, and recovers known bounds as special cases, while introducing a covariance-compatibility cost that captures the price of using ADAPTIVE reference geometries.

Abstract: Information-theoretic generalization bounds analyze stochastic optimization by relating expected generalization error to the mutual information between learned parameters and training data. Virtual perturbation analyses of SGD add auxiliary Gaussian noise only in the proof, making mutual information tractable while leaving the actual SGD trajectory unchanged. Existing bounds, however, typically require perturbation covariances to be fixed independently of the optimization history, limiting their ability to represent geometries induced by moving gradient statistics, preconditioners, curvature proxies, and other pathwise information.
  We introduce predictable history-adaptive virtual perturbations, where the perturbation covariance at each iteration may depend on the past real SGD history but not on current or future randomness. This predictability enables a conditional Gaussian relative-entropy argument and yields generalization bounds for SGD with adaptive virtual-noise geometry. The bounds replace fixed sensitivity and gradient-deviation terms with conditional adaptive counterparts, include an output-sensitivity penalty from accumulated perturbation covariance, and reduce the deviation term to a conditional variance only under conditional unbiasedness.
  Since adaptive covariances may be data-dependent, we separate local Gaussian smoothing from global reference-kernel comparison. The resulting bound includes a covariance-comparison cost measuring the KL price of using an admissible reference geometry different from the actual adaptive covariance. Fixed-noise-style bounds are recovered under admissible synchronization, such as deterministic, public, or prefix-observable covariance rules. The framework recovers fixed isotropic and geometry-aware bounds as special cases while extending virtual perturbation analysis to history-dependent SGD without modifying the algorithm.

### [91] [Hyperspherical Forward-Forward with Prototypical Representations](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00082)
*Shalini Sarode,Brian Moser,Joachim Folz,Federico Raue,Tobias Nauen,Stanislav Frolov,Andreas Dengel*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Abstract: The Forward-Forward (FF) algorithm presents a compelling, bio-inspired alternative to backpropagation. However, while efficient in training, it has a computationally prohibitive inference process that requires a separate forward pass for every class that is evaluated. In this work, we introduce the Hyperspherical Forward-Forward (HFF), a novel reformulation that resolves this critical bottleneck. Our core innovation is to reframe the local objective of each layer from a binary goodness-of-fit task to a direct multi-class classification problem within a hyperspherical feature space. We achieve this by learning a set of class-specific, unit-norm prototypes that act as geometric anchors and implicit negatives. This architectural innovation preserves the benefits of local training while enabling weight update and inference in a single forward pass, making it >40x faster than the original FF algorithm. Our method is simple to implement, scales effectively to modern convolutional architectures, and achieves superior accuracy on standard image classification benchmarks, closing the gap with backpropagation. Most notably, we are among the first greedy local-learning methods to report over 25% top-1 accuracy on ImageNet-1k, and 65.96% with transfer learning.

Method: An architecture that generates k fixed latent tokens via a cross-attention bottleneck. Trained with a dual-objective: (1) reconstruction loss ensuring tokens preserve information (sufficient statistics), and (2) a diversity penalty based on Total Coding Rate to minimize redundancy and promote disentanglement. The approach is framed as a Disentangled Rate-Distortion theory.

Existing self-supervised approaches, such as Masked Autoencoders (MAEs) are highly effective for pre-training general-purpose encoders. However, they do not explicitly learn compact and semantically interpretable latent representations, typically relying on heuristic aggregation strategies such as global average pooling or a designated [CLS] token. We propose a novel framework that compresses a variable-length MedTS into a fixed-size set of $k$ latent Fingerprint Tokens. Our architecture employs a cross-attention bottleneck to generate these tokens and is trained with a dual-objective function. The first objective is a reconstruction loss, which ensures the tokens are \textit{sufficient statistics} for the original data. The second, a diversity penalty based on the Total Coding Rate (TCR), explicitly minimizes the redundancy between tokens, encouraging them to become statistically \textit{disentangled} representations. We present the theoretical justification for our method, framing it as a novel \textbf{Disentangled Rate-Distortion} problem. This approach produces a low-dimensional, interpretable, and sample-efficient representation, where each token is encouraged to capture an independent factor of variation, paving the way for more robust digital biomarkers.

### [96] [Technical Report: Activation Residual Hessian Quantization (ARHQ) for Low-Bit LLM Quantization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00140)
*YiFeng Wang,Zhun Sun,Keisuke Sakaguchi*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: ARHQ is a post-training weight-splitting method for low-bit activation-weight quantization that uses an input-side residual Hessian to identify and isolate error-sensitive weight directions into a high-precision, low-rank branch via a closed-form truncated SVD of W G_x^{1/2}.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Error propagation in aggressive activation quantization degrades model accuracy; there is a need to protect weight directions that contribute most to quantization error.

Method: Construct an input-side residual Hessian G_x from activation quantization residuals; form the scaled weight matrix W G_x^{1/2}; compute a closed-form truncated SVD to separate error-sensitive directions into a high-precision, low-rank branch as part of a post-training weight-splitting scheme.

Result: On Qwen3-4B-Thinking-2507, ARHQ improves layer-wise SNR and preserves downstream reasoning performance on ZebraLogic under aggressive quantization; code is provided at the project URL.

Conclusion: ARHQ effectively mitigates error propagation in low-bit activation-weight quantization through targeted weight splitting, enabling better quantized-model performance and offering a practical PTQ technique with accessible implementation.

Abstract: We present Activation Residual Hessian Quantization (ARHQ), a post-training weight splitting method designed to mitigate error propagation in low-bit activation-weight quantization. By constructing an input-side residual Hessian from activation quantization residuals (G_x), ARHQ analytically identifies and isolates error-sensitive weight directions into a high-precision low-rank branch. This is achieved via a closed-form truncated SVD on the scaled weight matrix W G^{1/2}_x . Experimental results on Qwen3-4B-Thinking-2507 demonstrate that ARHQ significantly improves layer-wise SNR and preserves downstream reasoning performance on ZebraLogic even under aggressive quantization. The code is available at https://github.com/BeautMoonQ/ARHQ.

</details>


### [97] [Wasserstein Distributionally Robust Regret Optimization for Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00155)
*Yikai Wang,Shang Liu,Jose Blanchet*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: DRRO for RLHF optimizes regret under policy perturbations, providing a less pessimistic, tractable robustness framework that mitigates Goodharting more effectively than standard distributionally robust optimization (DRO) baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To address objective misspecification in RLHF, where the reward signal is a proxy for true human utility, leading to reward over-optimization (Goodharting). Conventional DRO is overly pessimistic and computationally burdensome; DRRO seeks a balance by optimizing regret relative to the best policy under plausible reward perturbations.

Method: Introduce Wasserstein distributionally robust regret optimization (DRRO) for RLHF. Formulate a simplex allocation (promptwise) model with an ℓ1 ambiguity set. Show that the inner worst-case regret has a closed-form solution via a water-filling structure. Derive a practical policy-gradient algorithm with a simple sampled-bonus interpretation and modest changes to PPO/GRPO-style training.

Result: DRRO yields a tractable optimization with a structured worst-case regret solution; empirically, it mitigates over-optimization (Goodharting) more effectively than existing baselines, while standard DRO remains overly pessimistic. The approach provides theoretical insight into why DRRO is less pessimistic and demonstrates practical applicability in RLHF pipelines.

Conclusion: DRRO offers a principled, less pessimistic robustness framework for RLHF that reduces reward misalignment due to objective misspecification. It delivers a practical training algorithm with modest computational overhead and improved performance against Goodharting compared to standard DRO-based methods.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) has become a core post-training step for aligning large language models, yet the reward signal used in RLHF is only a learned proxy for true human utility. From an operations research perspective, this creates a decision problem under objective misspecification: the policy is optimized against an estimated reward, while deployment performance is determined by an unobserved objective. The resulting gap leads to reward over-optimization, or Goodharting, where proxy reward continues to improve even after true quality deteriorates. Existing mitigations address this problem through uncertainty penalties, pessimistic rewards, or conservative constraints, but they can be computationally burdensome and overly pessimistic. We propose Wasserstein distributionally robust regret optimization (DRRO) for RLHF. Instead of pessimizing worst-case value as in standard DRO, DRRO pessimizes worst-case regret relative to the best policy under the same plausible reward perturbation. We study the promptwise problem through a simplex allocation model and show that, under an $\ell_1$ ambiguity set, the inner worst-case regret admits an exact solution and the optimal policy has a water-filling structure. These results lead to a practical policy-gradient algorithm with a simple sampled-bonus interpretation and only minor changes to PPO/GRPO-style RLHF training. The framework also clarifies theoretically why DRRO is less pessimistic than DRO, and our experiments show that DRRO mitigates over-optimization more effectively than existing baselines while standard DRO is systematically over-pessimistic.

For discrete diffusion, no analogous sample-space ODE exists, making direct adaptation ill-defined. We argue that the natural discrete substitute is not a deterministic trajectory but its stochastic counterpart: the exact posterior bridge, available in closed form for broad corruption families including masked and uniform diffusion. Building on this observation, we introduce Multi-Path Discrete Consistency (MPDC), a new principle that trains a denoiser to be path-invariant in expectation across these stochastic bridges, and instantiate it as the Consistent Diffusion Language Model (CDLM), a single-stage, teacher-free training framework. Empirically, CDLM establishes a new state of the art on both conditional and unconditional text-generation, consistently outperforming strong base discrete diffusion models and often even multi-stage distilled baselines across sampling budgets, with the largest gains in the few-step regime. Method: Formal analysis of subgroup predictive information; define a group-imbalance-agnostic barycenter that aggregates predictive information across all subgroups; modify the distillation objective to align representations toward this shared aggregate; ensure compatibility with existing distillation pipelines.

Abstract: Dataset Distillation aims to compress a large dataset into a small synthetic one while maintaining predictive performance. Consequently, models trained on distilled data can experience substantial performance drops for certain subgroups, leading to fairness gaps. Crucially, these gaps do not disappear by merely correcting group imbalance, since they stem from fundamental mismatches in subgroup predictive patterns rather than from sample-size disparities alone. We therefore formally analyze the interaction between these two sources of bias and cast the solution as identifying a group-imbalance-agnostic barycenter of the predictive information that induces similar representations across all subgroups. By distilling toward this shared aggregate representation, we show that group fairness concerns can be reduced. Our approach is compatible with existing distillation methods, and empirical results show that it substantially reduces bias introduced by dataset distillation.

Abstract: Bayesian optimization is a sequential method for minimizing objective functions that are expensive to evaluate and about which few assumptions can be made. ### [106] [NLPOpt-Net: A Learning Method for Nonlinear Optimization with Feasibility Guarantees](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00260)
*Bimol Nath Roy,Rahul Golder,MM Faruque Hasan*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: An unsupervised architecture (NLPOpt-Net) solves constrained nonlinear programs by combining a backbone neural network with a structured projection that enforces feasibility via local quadratic approximations; it uses an inversion-free Chambolle-Pock forward pass and implicit function theorem backpropagation, achieving near-zero optimality gap, machine-precision constraint satisfaction, accurate active sets, and fast inference with a ready-to-use GPU-enabled package.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To provide scalable, constraint-satisfying, parametric solutions for large-scale constrained optimization, addressing the need for fast inference and reliable feasibility in NLPs and related problems.

Method: A two-stage architecture: (1) a backbone neural network trained with a modified Lagrangian loss plus a consistency loss; (2) a fixed k-layer projection that enforces feasibility by projecting NN outputs onto the original constraint manifold using local quadratic approximations. The projection uses a modified Chambolle-Pock algorithm for forward passes and the implicit function theorem for backpropagation. Under convexity, the projection has a descent property. The projection is compiled in C for speed, enabling decoupled NN and projection after training, and supports GPU.

Result: Solves large-scale convex QP, QCQP, NLP, and some nonconvex problems with near-zero optimality gap and machine-precision constraint violations; yields near-accurate active-set predictions and dual variables; significant speedups from C compilation and GPU support; provides a ready-to-use package.

Conclusion: NLPOpt-Net offers a practical, efficient framework for parametric optimization of constrained problems, delivering accurate feasibility, competitive optimality, and scalable inference, with a ready-to-use GPU-enabled implementation.

Abstract: Nonlinear Parametric Optimization Network (NLPOpt-Net) is an unsupervised learning architecture to solve constrained nonlinear programs (NLP). Given the structure of an NLP, it learns the parametric solution maps with guaranteed constraint satisfaction. The architecture consists of a backbone neural network (NN) followed by a multilayer ($k$-layered) projection. While the NN drives toward optimality through a loss function consisting of a modified Lagrangian augmented with a consistency loss, the projection ensures feasibility by projecting the NN predictions in the original constraint manifold. Instead of typical distance minimization, our projection exploits local quadratic approximations of the original NLP. Under certain conditions (such as convexity), the projection has a descent property, which improves the NN predictions further. NLPOpt-Net deploys an inversion-free, modified Chambolle-Pock algorithm to solve the constrained quadratic projections during the forward pass and uses the implicit function theorem for efficient backpropagation. The fixed structure of the projection further allows decoupling of the NN and the projection once the training is complete. NLPOpt-Net solves large-scale convex QP, QCQP, NLP, and nonconvex problems with near zero optimality gap and constraint violations reduced to machine precision. Additionally, it provides near accurate prediction of the active sets and corresponding dual variables, thereby enabling a scalable approach for multiparametric programming. Compiling the projection in C provides order of magnitude improvement in inference time compared to JAX. We provide the codes and NLPOpt-Net as a ready to use package that includes GPU support.

### [108] [Polaris: Coupled Orbital Polar Embeddings for Hierarchical Concept Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.00265)

TL;DR: Polaris introduces a polar hyperspherical embedding that separates semantic meaning from hierarchy using angular geometry and radius, learning meaning and structure without interference. It projects latent representations onto the sphere via the tangent space at the north pole, applies the exponential map, and learns unit-norm embeddings with spherical linear layers. It combines robust local constraints, global regularization to prevent collapse, and uncertainty-aware asymmetric objectives, and uses structure-guided retrieval at inference. It achieves strong gains in taxonomy expansion across trees, DAGs, and multimodal hierarchies (up to ~19 point gains in top-K and ~60% reduction in mean rank over baselines).
